Being a recent fan of the [[ASIN:1934964573 Scott Pilgrim's Precious Little Boxset]] series of graphic novels by Bryan Lee O'Malley, I was looking forward to the film adaptation - especially after hearing of the casting (most notably, Michael Cera) and the director Edgar Wright ([[ASIN:B0025VLEM0 Shaun of the Dead [Blu-ray]]], [[ASIN:B002HR1W1K Hot Fuzz (Ultimate Edition) [Blu-ray]]]). I'm not the biggest fan of Wright's work; while I thought they were slightly funny films, I don't fall into the large masses who say they're the most hilarious contemporary films. Still, I like Wright's filmmaking style and I knew he'd do a great job with the material.<br /><br />There are quite a few people who believe Michael Cera acts the same in every movie and TV show he is in. And, depending on the actor, that can either be OK or extremely annoying. Cera does generally have the same acting methods, but, to me, it works for him. He has mastered the act of shy-but-hilarious, slightly neurotic geek, and I never tire of - or fail to laugh at - any role he portrays. And I'm a firm believer in that saying: if it ain't broke, don't fix it! The bottom line is: if you despise Cera then don't see this film as you'll probably only be rolling your eyes through most of it since it's his movie, and this film deserves more than that.<br /><br />I know people may laugh at the thought of a bestselling memoir geared towards women (\\"Eat Pray Love\\") being compared to a bestselling graphic novel geared towards male adolescents. But, trust me, their meanings are similar. As I stated in my review for \\"Eat Pray Love,\\" it's a story about a writer taking a quest in Italy, India and Bali to forget her problems, start anew, and experience flavor and deep contemplation only to realize that you can't run from who you are; you have to be comfortable with who you are and make peace with what's happened in your life ... the good and the bad ... whether it was your doing or someone else's. It's no big surprise spoiler that Julia Roberts' Gilbert finds love and self-respect for herself and, at the same time, makes peace with the hurtful choices she's made. Now onto Cera's Scott Pilgrim. Although it's more prevalent in the books, the viewer can also tell in the film that Pilgrim is not only a slacker financially, but also in self-respect and accountability. He's made bad, selfish choices in love and he takes the universal young generation response whenever it comes to any sort of confrontation: \\"whatever.\\" Nevertheless, I found his character much more likeable than Roberts' Gilbert.<br /><br />The film, \\"Scott Pilgrim\\" is told in a visual way that has the style format of a video game and comic book with the main character - finding himself having to fight - oops! - I mean, defeat the seven evil ex-boyfriends of his newest love interest, Ramona Flowers (Mary Elizabeth Winstead). It's all metaphor for the baggage we carry when dating someone. Not only does one deal with the issues of our significant other's romantic past (how it affects them emotionally), but you also deal with your own insecurities and vulnerability when entering into a relationship and - unless your beloved has lived in a cave and had no prior romantic relationships - how your spouse's past suitors measure up to you. It's a silly, self-destructive game we play in our love lives, but everyone does it ... especially more frequently when we're younger.<br /><br />SPOILER ALERT!<br />It's only when Pilgrim confronts his unresolved issues and finds self-respect that he truly wins \\"the game.\\" And the girl. We see the transformation of a somewhat judgmental guy turn into a man who can love someone for who they truly are, including their flaws.<br />SPOILER ENDS!<br /><br />As I said, the film, like its literary original, utilizes the comic panel sound effects of BAM, WHOOSH, etc., and cool, quirky characters, as well as the video game staples of coin dispersal after defeating an opponent, earning an extra life and special weapons, the countdown to continue a game, and the versus fight mode including fight combos and booming voice-over narration of \\"K.O.!\\" It's a unique, fresh style that I'm surprised hasn't been attempted before. Don't mistake me for some diehard gamer; even though I owned an original Xbox, I haven't been heavily involved with video games since the original Nintendo. Regardless, I loved the style Wright brought to the screen thanks to creator O'Malley. Second to Robert Rodriguez's film adaptation of Frank Miller's [[ASIN:1593963149 Frank Miller's Complete Sin City Library]] ([[ASIN:B001R4KQDE Sin City (Two-Disc Theatrical & Recut, Extended, and Unrated Versions) [Blu-ray]]]), this is the closest the viewer will get to a comic book come to life! And while that sounds spectacular to some; there will be others that find the movie just plain weird. It is weird. But it's a weird that's kind of great. It has wonderful meaning and great depth underneath the generation-X trappings of vegans, garage bands, cheat codes, straight edge, and \\"whatever.\\"<br /><br />I was surprised to love the book as much I do because it's made up of what I mostly dislike with comics: it resembles a manga (I despise those!), it's black-and-white, and the art is more cartoonish rather than realistic (although, it does grow on you). Since I do love it, though, it's no surprise I enjoyed the film. It was wonderful seeing all the dialogue and one-liners from the books actually being said out loud, facial expressions and all. Cera, Winstead, and Kieran Culkin stand out in the film, while the evil exes each do good at providing humor while simultaneously presenting to Scott with a different issue or concern regarding his blossoming relationship with Ramona; but Scott's not the only one who has to confront issues, Ramona also finds herself having to face Scott's baggage. The [[ASIN:B003SG810Y soundtrack]] and score are impressive and I like them but I can see how it's not as appealable to a wide audience as the [[ASIN:B002BAODSC (500) Days Of Summer-Music From The Motion Picture]] (the best track on Pilgrim's soundtrack is Metric's \\"Black Sheep,\\" which is used as a song sung by The Clash at Demonhead). The songs all work splendidly in the film - i.e., when Ramona drives off with evil ex Gideon, the Rolling Stones' \\"Under My Thumb\\" is appropriately being played on the car stereo (check out the lyrics to know just how appropriate it is). Lastly, for a story that took six books to tell, it's expected that not everything from those books will be included in the film. In most films adapted from books, it seems most screenwriters leave out the best parts and add in the most lame. Don't fret, though. Screenwriters Wright and Michael Bacall kept in all of the cool lines and important scenes central to the story of Scott and Ramona; some lines are mixed with different scenes compared to the books, but it all still works out really well and, overall, stays true to the books (i.e., in the books, Ramona fights Scott's ex, Envy, but in the film, Ramona fights one of her evil exes, Roxy). Some funny parts fall flat but it's few and far between.<br /><br />\\"Scott Pilgrim vs. the World\\" is a fun, weird, fresh, innovative movie that's destined for cult status. Take that as you want, but, to this critic, I thoroughly enjoyed it!ike its literary original, utilizes the comic panel sound effects of BAM, WHOOSH, etc., and cool, quirky characters, as well as the video game staples of coin dispersal after defeating an opponent, earning an extra life and special weapons, the countdown to continue a game, and the versus fight mode including fight combos and booming voice-over narration of \\"K.O.!\\" It's a unique, fresh style that I'm surprised hasn't been attempted before. Regardless, I loved the style Wright brought to the screen thanks to creator O'Malley. And while that sounds spectacular to some; there will be others that find the movie just plain weird. It is weird. But it's a weird that's kind of great. 0765350386|3.0|If nothing else, Sanderson's Mistborn is fun. The action is interesting, the world is detailed, and magic system is unique and intriguing. Its main character is a teenage girl named Vin who is magically gifted, being able to not only control the emotions of others, but also able to fight with superhuman powers. She learns of her magic powers through the aid of Kelsier, another magically gifted individual, who needs Vin to overthrow the oppressive government. It's a neat, though not original, premise. The action is fast, thankfully, and there are only a few dull moments.     Mistborn, however fun it is, is not a deep read and did not hold my interest after I finished reading (that is, I did not go out and buy the next volume in the series). The characters are shallow and wherever Sanderson tries to impart depth, it comes out as melodramatic. This is partly due to my having read so much fantasy that nothing comes across as new. It is also due to Sanderson's writing: it lacks subtlety. Sanderson tells the reader exactly how each character is feeling and thinking in fine detail, so the reader does not have an opportunity to think for himself. Instead of using dialogue to clue the reader of emotions, the dialogue itself is injected with narrative that lays everything bare.     Still, Mistborn is a fun read and the best thing I can say is that it was not a waste of time.

0393327655|3.0|It isn't surprising that many of the reviews for The End of Faith do not provide potential readers with much insight into the merits (or lack thereof) of the book in question.  Based on the reviews posted it would appear that the book is more of a catalyst for discussion about the issue of faith.  This isn't a bad thing; in fact the author's intention is clearly to spark public discourse on the issue of faith.  I will inevitably jump into the fray in this regard as well.  What I will try to do first though is offer an opinion on the book and try to help potential readers decide if this is something they might want to read.<br /><br />I've read a fair number of books on early Christianity and religion written by atheist or agnostic authors.  The books that focus on the history of our monotheistic religions tend to be the most objective and the most interesting in terms of learning about the early days of these religions.  Books like The End of Faith are a different breed.  The authors are generally making social commentary.  These types of books tend to attract people who already share the view of the author (to use an ironic comparison: these authors inevitably preach to the choir) or those who are already diametrically opposed to the author's view who read the book (or not) for the sole purpose of writing a scathing review.  Interestingly, most of these books are written as if specifically addressing the people in the middle (that is, religious moderates).<br /><br />In reality, I don't think many religious moderates are likely to read or enjoy a book like this.  Most religious moderates are only occasional readers of their own religious texts and, in my opinion, unlikely to seek out books debating the merits of religion.  Generally speaking, I think most religious moderates are content in their own vague beliefs and are disinclined to apply a great deal of thought as to whether or not their religious convictions are valid.  So while the book is written, in a sense, for them - I don't think it's likely to appeal.  Harris is just too aggressive in presenting his point of view and uses turns of phrase that are likely to turn off any moderate.  Obviously, deeply religious people are not going to be convinced to abandon their faith by any of the arguments presented and should they choose to read the book is likely only so that they can express their outrage in a review.<br /><br />So, I think this is a book that will pretty much only appeal to atheists.  Even then, not all atheists are going to be enamored by it.  I think Harris has two valid points that he raises in this book.  Are these points sufficient to support a whole book though?  I'm inclined to think - no.  Not really.  There's just not enough here to sustain the page count in The End of Faith.  As a result Harris rambles on, gets much too heavy handed in places and even comes across as hypocritical at times.  I found Harris' \\"Letter to a Christian Nation\\" much more focused and effective.  I'm the choir, but I can only give this book 3 stars.<br /><br />Now, like everyone else, I do want to expound on two key messages in the book.  The first is that the taboos around criticizing religion need to be removed.  Religion does not belong in the same category as race, sex, and sexual orientation.  Religion is a belief system - it's an opinion.  That's all.  People's religion should be treated the same as their political affiliation. You have a right to an opinion as to the existence of God in the same manner that you have a right to an opinion about health care spending.  And I should have the right to be critical of it.<br /><br />Unfortunately, to criticize someone's religion is viewed as intolerant, as if one were criticizing someone's race or sex.  This is a problem.  We need to protect a person's right to their religion, we shouldn't discriminate - but we should have every right to say that we disagree with it and frankly that we oppose it, without being viewed as intolerant.<br /><br />The second point that I think Harris makes in his book that is worth emphasizing is that those who use the words in these religious texts to harm others, be it through warfare, terrorism, persecution, or the stripping away of civil rights, are not misinterpreting their ancient books.  These books may contain the golden rule and periodic references to respecting `the people of the book' but they also contain frequent, unambiguous references to killing those who commit minor offenses and in particular for those attempting to lead believers astray from their god.  The Quran in particular contains repeated references to conquering, subjugating, and exterminating infidels and promises rewards after death to those who die fighting for their god.  The single passage that instructs believers not to destroy themselves is countered by repeated references to the glory of dying while fighting for god.  It is a common misconception that these religions are not violent or intolerant and that those who use these texts to promote violence and intolerance are `extremists' distorting the true nature of their religion.  The reality is that these are ancient religions established in a far less enlightened time that promote murder, homophobia, subjugation of women, and genocide.  These texts are not being misinterpreted by extremists - it is that religious moderates are choosing to ignore the parts of their religious texts that are not palatable to modern living.<br /><br />In closing I would like to say that I think of religion in much the same way I regard fairies and smoking.  I don't believe fairies exist but I remain open-minded.  If provided with evidence to suggest that fairies actually do exist, I would change my position depending on how persuasive the new information is.  In the absence of evidence, particularly in light of the extraordinary nature of the claim, I am not a believer in fairies.  I don't smoke and I'm opposed to smoking but I support your right to smoke as much and as often as you want, so long it doesn't interfere with a non-smokers right not to breathe your smoke.I think Harris makes in his book that is worth emphasizing is that those who use the words in these religious texts to harm others, be it through warfare, terrorism, persecution, or the stripping away of civil rights, are not misinterpreting their ancient books. B00000DHR0|3.0|Whether you think of Celine Dion as exquisite or just excessive, you still gotta admit that there's no singer alive who's more suited to doing a Christmas album--that is, if you consider the genre in more or less pop-conventional terms.  Holiday records are actually kind of problematic for most contemporary recording artists.  The thing that seems to bog down so many performers who attempt a \\"seasonal\\" record is that they try to have it both ways.  They want to be a little sentimental, but also feel the need to be knowing and ironic.  There's always that tension between the holy and the hip.    Whatever else you might want to say about Dion, you gotta admit--for ill or good--she doesn't have an IRONIC bone in her body, especially when she's singing in English.  She grew up listening to and absorbing all kinds of music from the lower 48, but like many non-US born singers who tackle American music, she can't quite get that attitude thing down.  Even the schmaltziest American artists are \\"aware\\" of the schmaltz.  They acknowledge their own cheesiness and make gentle fun of themselves in their stage banter, their phrasing and their demeanor.  When they drag out the holiday chestnuts, there's a tendency to sing 'em with a nod and a wink, even as they're roasting 'em over an open fire.    That's pretty much a non-issue as far as Mme Angelil is concerned.  The holiday numbers on this record are sung as \\"straight\\" and as SINCERELY as you're going to find a contemporary diva do them.  Whether you find her takes on such carols as \\"O Holy Night,\\" or \\"O Come All Ye Faithful,\\" or the aria \\"Ave Maria\\" tasteful over or under the top, you gotta admit she's right out there, with her heart on the sleeve of her holiday sweater.  My own take is that these tracks have some lovely moments AND some of sheer overkill.  I find it makes a big difference what system I play this record on.  On my car stereo--which usually sounds pretty good--the record, and some of Celine Dion's high notes, sound so distorted  and so nasal that I have to tune the volume down to next to nothing.  Or just turn the dang thing off.    But give Celine (or her producers) some credit.  Not every track is a tour-de-force, in fact.  Some like her take on \\"Blue Christmas\\" are relatively understated.  And since no Xmas album is complete without a few surprises, I give her her props for finding (and in one case co-writing) some pleasant, radio-friendly holiday tunes that are a bit off the beaten track (or should I say, \\"the well trodden snow covered lane\\"?).   Her \\"Don't Save It All For Christmas Day\\" is just such a pleasant surprise, a little soulful but relatively restrained. You can pretty much say the same for D. Snyder's \\"Magic of Christmas Day.\\"  Bryan Adams \\"Another Year Has Gone By\\" is not, strictly speaking, a straight holiday tune (it mentions the 4th of July, as well as Christmas) but it's an \\"end of year\\" song (although it COULD actually refer to a couple's anniversary date and not the end of the calendar year).  Thematically, it fits in fairly well nonetheless--which is probably the rationale behind the \\"sorta\\" title tune. The holidays are a \\"special time,\\" but they aren't specifically the \\"special times\\" cited in the song.  Nothing really glaringly out of place though.    Not quite as sure how you can readily justify the inclusion of \\"Brahm's Lullabye\\" in the mix here--well, babies and Christmas, yeah, I guess they all make for \\"special times,\\" but I can imagine that a few fans would have preferred a more standard holiday blend.  And too, her two duets are not really holiday fare, although the number she shares with Andrea Bocelli, \\"Prayer,\\" is at least thematically relgious.  \\"I'm Your Angel,\\" her duet with R. Kelly, could have been a track on any of her standard pop albums.  I won't even try to justify it with a \\"Christmas/Angel\\" connection.  Actually, it just seems reflective of a recent trend in holiday pop albums to throw in a few non-seasonal numbers, probably tokeep 'em listening well beyond the holidays.       When Celine Dion tackles an iconic rocker's song, she opens herself up for all kinds of criticism.  I don't know that she has a clue about the bitter undertone in John Lennon's \\"Happy Xmas (War Is Over).\\"  Lennon just about spat out the lines, \\"And so this is Christmas/And what have you done.\\"  The song is as bitter as it is hopeful, perhaps more of a wish to be able to BELIEVE in peace and love than an expression of same.  I don't think Celine quite gets that. But after a few listenings, I decided the world had room for her decidedly non-ironic take on the song.  I'll admit though, the first few times had me missing Yoko Ono.    I gather some die hard fans were disappointed with the albums closing tracks, in which Celine is joined by her famously musical family.  Things get all universal and multi-cultural on \\"Feliz Navidad,\\" with her French speaking family members joining in the chorus of alternating Spanish and English text.  Gotta love the universalism in that.  But, even though I know I'm a minority here, for me the highlight of the reocrd is the atypical closing number, \\"Les Cloches du Hameau,\\" in which Celine's is just one of many voices in the family choir.  No diva-nonsense among the old folks at home.  Kind of refreshing when you think of it.

Each year the government chooses a boy and girl from each district to participate in the Hunger Games - a fight to the death, televised for all to see. The Hunger Games are a punishment on the districts for a long-ago uprising against the Capitol. This year, Katniss is shocked when her young sister's name is chosen to participate from the lottery. So Katniss volunteers to take her place, along with Peeta - the son of the district's baker. The two have little fighting skills compared to other districts' participants who have been training their whole lives for this event.<br /><br />Katniss is a completely engaging character. I usually can't stand present tense narrative, but Collins' writing pulled me in nonetheless. I was pulled in from the start, and couldn't let go. It's a story of love, friendship, surviving, violence, and death. The reader experiences everything that Katniss does, and experiences a full range of emotions. Collins' characters are truly stunning.<br /><br />This is an immensely popular trilogy, and I now know why. With an abundance of drama and suspense, this futuristic science fiction novel for young adults will appeal to readers young and old. It's breathtaking and a fantastic adventure from beginning to end.

0439023483|5.0|Firstly, if it were possible, this book would be a four and a half star review. It lost half a star due to the ending feeling rushed and I dislike that in a great book that you're immersed in, otherwise, it's deserving of a five star review and here's why.    The Hunger Games is the most unique concept thought up that I have ever read. Who would think that culling your children for sport would ever happen, yet when you think about it, look at the world - child soldiers anyone? However this is more big brother meets survival on your television screen. This concept could be gory, yet with death scenes the author shows compassion and grace, after all, it's not like anyone volunteers to be a tribute representing their district in the games, unless you're saving a member of your family.     Heartwarming probably isn't a word you'd use given the topic, however Katniss, our leading lady, shows such humility to her fellow tributes that she becomes a district wide hero, and that then makes her a governmental enemy. How dare she have everyone liking her. It's supposed to be entertainment, not a show of mercy and compassion. Yet it's these small gestures that make the book real. How can she kill someone that is just like her sister and not be affected? Assuming she does... And of course such actions bring repercussions. Breaking the rules brings consequence.    You cannot read this book without being part of it. Without wondering how their parents are thinking as they watch their children die. Without wondering what horrible theme is going to be set upon the tributes in the games. It's certainly not an honour to win, after all, that means you've survived where as 23 others haven't. Their mentor - a former tribute winner - is now a raging alcoholic. This is what the games do to you it seems.    There are political plans, romance, family, and a huge dose of do whatever you must to get you through, and there are gory moments yet they don't read as some horror gore, they read as survival, with respect. Props to the author for such genuine writing and for creating characters who are so real, with brains, hearts, individual talents, and filling the book with humanity - giving the world, our world, a taste of what we could become, and probably are, like.    The hunger games was well written, compelling, intelligent, unique, and I couldn't put it down. As I said, my only irk with it was that the ending was suddenly rushed and to me, didn't fit the pace with the rest of the novel. But don't let that stop you. This is one worth reading.

0525951652|5.0|When it comes to dramatizing history, no one - and I mean no one - does it better than Ken Follett. With no less than a dozen main characters spread across the globe, Follett pulls off the amazing feat of making each character's storyline as compelling as the next. So much so that not once did I find myself bored or frustrated, wishing I could jump ahead. Whether it's the poor Russian peasant dreaming of life in America, the idealistic young American aid to a President on the brink of war, the beautiful English house servant in love with her master, or the good-hearted German Baron torn between conscience and country, the drama in their personal lives beautifully reflects the drama unfolding in the geo-political world around them. If you're looking for a dry, sober account of the events that lead to the first and second world wars with no humanistic take whatsoever... buy yourself a history book. But if you want an informative, romantic and engrossing epic that truly makes you feel what it was like to have lived through the events that would define the 20th century, than this is the book for you.    P.S. For those who take issue with the fictional side of historical fiction, think of it like this... Films like LAWRENCE OF ARABIA, BRAVEHEART, THE GODFATHER, or TV shows like \\"Deadwood,\\" \\"Mad Men,\\" \\"Boardwalk Empire,\\" and \\"The Tudors,\\" while far from accurate, have successfully introduced millions to important moments in history they otherwise would never have known about. And at the very least, inspiried them to look up and learn more about these people, places and times of the past that helped shape our present.

0786955554|5.0|As a long-time collector of Dungeon Tiles I can say without reservation that this is the best collection yet published. What is this thing?:  Dungeon Tiles are handsomely detailed pre-printed heavy cardstock 'pieces' that you can use to cobble together a map for wargames.  In this case the tiles are sized for D&D miniatures (~25-30mm), with the grid spacing hovering around an inch.  The Dungeon Tiles sets include a whole pile of tiles representing rooms, corridors, furniture, traps, and the like.      The Tiles:  The Dungeon Tiles Master Set - The Dungeon contains a smrgsbord (a lot!) of tiles collected from previous editions, enough to build a respectable multi-room dungeon out of.  With a full ten sheets of double-sided tiles this is the biggest single collection published.      The Box:  In addition, the oversized box the tiles ship in provides superior protection to the tiles within, in addition to being a giant tile itself (the box is printed like a dungeon plinth and made from the same materials as the tiles themselves).  The box is easily big enough to store any loose tiles you might have collected from previous sets too, at least twice as thick as necessary for the product within.    The Value:  There has never been a better value in dungeon tiles published by Wizards of the Coast, between the number of tiles for your money, as well as the very handy box, this is a fantastic way for new DMs to build their collection of handy tiles.    Altogether this is a wonderful product for new DMs, or for those who wish to expand their collections.  If you already own several copies of every other Wizards produced Dungeon Tile you will not find anything new here beyond the box.

The three separate love stories follow the lives of generally unnoticed Jubilee, witty and funny Tobin, and generally bubbly, but now a little gloomy, Addie. We find each of them on Christmas Eve when one of the biggest storms hits Gracetwon, Virginia.     We find Jubilee, who is currently dating the \\"perfect\\" guy, stuck in Gracetown on her way to Florida. She happens to meet a young man named Stuart, who is kind of mysterious, but sweet and kind at the same time. Next we meet Tobin for our second story, who is currently enrolled in a movie night with his two best friends. Finally in our third story we meet Addie, recently broken up with her soul mate, she is trying to find her way again, but it may not be all that for her in the future.      John Green, Maureen Johnson, and Lauren Myracle create a book with and extremely unique style and sense of humor. If you enjoy sweet heartfelt love stories, this book is definitely what you are looking for. This book is so well thought out and so well developed that it is amazing, and I completely recommend this book.

0307341569|5.0|Flynn has an eye for atmospheric detail and an ear for perfect-pitch dialogue and alternative speech patterns that pulls in the reader from the first page. Each character is layered with distinct moods, motives and experiences that even when shared are misunderstood or unknown to the others. This approach propels this complicated story full throttle. But, Libby Day is the most interesting narrator, who stands out as the most realistic and engaging. Thus, she saves the story from being overshadowed by the too many desperate, and mostly despicable, people that populate the book. Reminiscent of Capote's \\"In Cold Blood,\\" the plot centers on the massacre of the Day family on a Kansas farm. In both stories, the murders are committed seemingly without reason or forethought. There the comparison ends: \\"In Cold Blood\\" tells the real murder of a prominent \\"normal\\" family; \\"Dark Places\\" describes a family, marginalized and dysfunctional, to whom nothing good has happened in a long time. Lest you think the difference in the stories is the product of fiction, at several points, Flynn reminds us of how society treats the not-pretty, not-rich. In \\"Dark Places,\\" true justice is not sought in behalf of the dead - or living - Days. Ben, the 15-year old son is accused and convicted of being the sole killer of his family. Getting to the night of the carnage is a rough, compelling read through grisly scenes. Even with the unexpected closing, this story of loss, punishment and atonement left me feeling that the situations were unavoidable.  From the beginning, there was no one to save the Day family.

0345505387|3.0|I just received this graphic novel about 2 hours ago..and now writing a so so review, unfortunately.  I knew what to expect, from keeping up with her website.   Graphic novels are just that..comicbook-like stories.  A few things:  1 - The artwork at first and the renditions of the characters were amazing.  I said..Yes!!  That is exactly how I pictured Jamie, Murtagh, Claire, Mrs. Fitz...etc.   Then, the faces changed...and badly.  By the end of the novel, Jamie looked swollen-faced...where were the high cheek bones, the slanted BLUE eyes, the straight nose...did he gain weight?  And, Claire's hair was suddenly less frizzy in the wet Scottish weather...quite the opposite from what the book depicts over and over again - her unruly curls.  So, those facts took away from the story for me.  If their looks had been the slightest bit consistent throughout the novel, my interest would have been better kept.  Not being an expert in the least on graphic novels, I'd venture a guess that features are supersized and over emphasized.  Claire's figure got more volumptuous as the story went on it seemed.      2 - I know DG mentioned that the story was different somewhat - and deliberately..but who is that Kenneth character? I had to go back a few pages just to figure him out. Did Murtagh know him...how did Geillie know him?  And, if we didn't know that Claire time-travelled, couldn't she have explained it to Jamie a bit more...before he suddenly believed her and took her back to the stones?  Should we expect The Exile - part 2 soon?         I am a huge Outlander fan, and I think DG is a master writer.  This foray into graphic novels is great for her and her fans...the idea was a good one for us to have another \\"taste\\" of Outlander without having to wait umpteen years in-between.  However, the finished package...was disappointing.
